States</a></td></tr><tr style="display:none"><th colspan="2">\n</th></tr><tr style="display:none"><th colspan="2">\n</th></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Carthage</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/&#712;/: primary stress follows">&#712;</span><span title="\'k\' in \'kind\'">k</span><span title="/&#593;&#720;r/: \'ar\' in \'far\'">&#593;&#720;r</span><span title="/&#952;/: \'th\' in \'thigh\'">&#952;</span><span title="/&#618;/: \'i\' in \'kit\'">&#618;</span><span title="/d&#658;/: \'j\' in \'jam\'">d&#658;</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Punic_language" title="Punic language">Punic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="xpu">&#67858;&#67859;&#67861;&#8226;&#67847;&#67843;&#67860;&#67861;</span>, <i><i lang="phn-Latn" title="Phoenician language text">Qart-&#7717;ada&#353;t</i></i>, "New City"; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">Carth&#257;g&#333;</i>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">&#1602;&#1585;&#1591;&#1575;&#1580;</span>&#8206;, <i>Qar&#7789;&#257;j</i>) was the center or capital city of the ancient <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Carthage" title="Ancient Carthage">Carthaginian civilization</a>, on the eastern side of the <a href="/wiki/Lake_of_Tunis" title="Lake of Tunis">Lake of Tunis</a>  in what is now the <a href="/wiki/Tunis_Governorate" title="Tunis Governorate">Tunis Governorate</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>.\n</p><p>The city developed from a <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenician</a> colony into the capital of a <a href="/wiki/Punics" title="Punics">Punic</a> empire dominating the Mediterranean during the first millennium BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> The legendary Queen <a href="/wiki/Dido" title="Dido">Dido</a> is regarded as the founder of the city, though her historicity has been questioned. According to accounts by <a href="/wiki/Timaeus_(historian)" title="Timaeus (historian)">Timaeus</a> of <a href="/wiki/Taormina" title="Taormina">Tauromenium</a>, she purchased from a local tribe the amount of land that could be covered by an oxhide. Cutting the skin into strips, she laid out her claim and founded an empire that would become, through the <a href="/wiki/Punic_Wars" title="Punic Wars">Punic Wars</a>, the only existential threat to <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Rome</a> until the coming of the <a href="/wiki/Vandals" title="Vandals">Vandals</a> several centuries later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The ancient city was <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Carthage_(c._149_BC)" title="Battle of Carthage (c. 149 BC)">destroyed</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> in the  <a href="/wiki/Third_Punic_War" title="Third Punic War">Third Punic War</a> in 146 BC and then re-developed as <a href="/wiki/Roman_Carthage" title="Roman Carthage">Roman Carthage</a>, which became the major city of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> in the province of <a href="/wiki/Africa_(Roman_province)" title="Africa (Roman province)">Africa</a>. The Roman city was again occupied by the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_the_Maghreb" title="Muslim conquest of the Maghreb">Muslim conquest of the Maghreb</a> in 698. The site remained uninhabited, the regional power shifting to the <a href="/wiki/Medina_of_Tunis" title="Medina of Tunis">Medina of Tunis</a> in the medieval period, until the early 20th century, when it began to develop into a coastal suburb of Tunis, incorporated as <a href="/wiki/Carthage_(municipality)" title="Carthage (municipality)">Carthage municipality</a> in 1919.\n</p><p>The archaeological site was first surveyed in 1830, by Danish consul <a href="/wiki/Christian_Tuxen_Falbe" title="Christian Tuxen Falbe">Christian Tuxen Falbe</a>. Excavations were performed in the second half of the 19th century by \n<a href="/wiki/Charles_Ernest_Beul%C3%A9" title="Charles Ernest Beul&#233;">Charles Ernest Beul&#233;</a> and by <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Louis_Delattre" title="Alfred Louis Delattre">Alfred Louis Delattre</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Carthage_National_Museum" title="Carthage National Museum">Carthage National Museum</a> was founded in 1875 by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardinal_(Catholicism)" title="Cardinal (Catholicism)">Cardinal</a> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lavigerie" title="Charles Lavigerie">Charles Lavigerie</a>.\nExcavations performed by French archaeologists in the 1920s first attracted an extraordinary amount of attention because of the evidence they produced for <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Carthage#Child_sacrifice" title="Religion in Carthage">child sacrifice</a>. Although there has been considerable disagreement among scholars concerning whether or not child sacrifice was practiced by ancient Carthage,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> recent research indicates that child sacrifice was in practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sacrifice1_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-sacrifice1-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sacrifice2_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-sacrifice2-6">[6]</a></sup> The open-air <a href="/wiki/Carthage_Paleo-Christian_Museum" title="Carthage Paleo-Christian Museum">Carthage Paleo-Christian Museum</a> has exhibits excavated under the auspices of <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> from 1975 to 1984.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Contemporary sources</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carthage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p><span id="Etymology"></span>\n</p>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia#Etymology" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicia &#167;&#160;Etymology</a></div>\n<p>The name <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Carthage" title="wikt:Carthage">Carthage</a></i> /&#712;kar&#952;&#618;d&#658;/ is the <a href="/wiki/Early_Modern_English" title="Early Modern English">Early Modern</a> <a href="/wiki/Anglicisation#Anglicisation_of_non-English_place_names" title="Anglicisation">anglicisation</a> of French <i>Carthage</i> /ka&#641;.ta&#658;/,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> from <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Carth&#257;g&#333;</i></i> and <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Karth&#257;g&#333;</i></i> (cf. <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> <i>Karkh&#275;d&#333;n</i> (<span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#922;&#945;&#961;&#967;&#951;&#948;&#974;&#957;</span>) and <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_language" title="Etruscan language">Etruscan</a> <i>*Car&#952;aza</i>) from the <a href="/wiki/Punic_language" title="Punic language">Punic</a> <i><i lang="phn-Latn" title="Phoenician language text">qrt-&#7717;d&#353;t</i></i> <span class="nowrap">(<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r866749807">.mw-parser-output .script-phoenician{font-family:"ALPHABETUM Unicode","MPH 2B Damase",Aegean,Code2001,"Noto Sans Phoenician",FreeSans,"Segoe UI Historic",sans-serif}</style><span class="script-phoenician" dir="rtl" title="Phoenician alphabet">&#67858;&#67859;&#67861; &#67847;&#67843;&#67860;&#67861;</span>&#8236;)</span> "new city",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> implying it was a "new <a href="/wiki/Tyre,_Lebanon" title="Tyre, Lebanon">Tyre</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The Latin adjective <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/punicus#Latin" title="wikt:punicus">p&#363;nicus</a></i>, meaning "Phoenician", is reflected in English in some borrowings from Latin&#8212;notably the <a href="/wiki/Punic_Wars" title="Punic Wars">Punic Wars</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Punic_language" title="Punic language">Punic language</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Modern_Standard_Arabic" title="Modern Standard Arabic">Modern Standard Arabic</a> form <span dir="rtl" lang="ar" title="Arabic language text">&#1602;&#1585;&#1591;&#1575;&#1580;</span> (<i><span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:9artaj.wav" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b9/9artaj.wav" title="9artaj.wav">Qar&#7789;&#257;j</a></span></span></i>) is an adoption of French <i>Carthage</i>, replacing an older local toponym reported as <i>Cartagenna</i> that directly continued the Latin name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Topography">Topography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carthage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Topography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carthage_archaeological_sites_map-fr.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="880" data-file-width="1030" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Carthage_archaeological_sites_map-fr.svg/220px-Carthage_archaeological_sites_map-fr.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Carthage_archaeological_sites_map-fr.svg/330px-Carthage_archaeological_sites_map-fr.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Carthage_archaeological_sites_map-fr.svg/440px-Carthage_archaeological_sites_map-fr.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carthage_archaeological_sites_map-fr.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Archaeological map</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Archaeological_Site_of_Carthage-130237.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3240" data-file-width="4320" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Archaeological_Site_of_Carthage-130237.jpg/220px-Archaeological_Site_of_Carthage-130237.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Archaeological_Site_of_Carthage-130237.jpg/330px-Archaeological_Site_of_Carthage-130237.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Archaeological_Site_of_Carthage-130237.jpg/440px-Archaeological_Site_of_Carthage-130237.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Archaeological_Site_of_Carthage-130237.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Archaeological Site of Carthage</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Archaeological_Site_of_Carthage-130238.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3240" data-file-width="4320" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Archaeological_Site_of_Carthage-130238.jpg/220px-Archaeological_Site_of_Carthage-130238.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Archaeological_Site_of_Carthage-130238.jpg/330px-Archaeological_Site_of_Carthage-130238.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Archaeological_Site_of_Carthage-130238.jpg/440px-Archaeological_Site_of_Carthage-130238.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Archaeological_Site_of_Carthage-130238.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Archaeological Site of Carthage</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Archaeological_Site_of_Carthage-130239.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4320" data-file-width="3240" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Archaeological_Site_of_Carthage-130239.jpg/220px-Archaeological_Site_of_Carthage-130239.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Archaeological_Site_of_Carthage-130239.jpg/330px-Archaeological_Site_of_Carthage-130239.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Archaeological_Site_of_Carthage-130239.jpg/440px-Archaeological_Site_of_Carthage-130239.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Archaeological_Site_of_Carthage-130239.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>View of two columns at Carthage</div></div></div>\n<p>Carthage was built on a <a href="/wiki/Promontory" title="Promontory">promontory</a> with sea inlets to the north and the south. The city\'s location made it master of the Mediterranean\'s maritime trade. All ships crossing the sea had to pass between <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> and the coast of Tunisia, where Carthage was built, affording it great power and influence. Two large, artificial harbors were built within the city, one for harboring the city\'s massive navy of 220 warships and the other for mercantile trade. A walled tower overlooked both harbors. The city had massive walls, 37&#160;km (23&#160;mi) in length, longer than the walls of comparable cities. Most of the walls were located on the shore, thus could be less impressive, as Carthaginian control of the sea made attack from that direction difficult. The 4.0 to 4.8&#160;km (2.5 to 3&#160;mi) of wall on the <a href="/wiki/Isthmus" title="Isthmus">isthmus</a> to the west were truly massive and were never penetrated. The city had a huge <a href="/wiki/Necropolis" title="Necropolis">necropolis</a> or burial ground, religious area, market places, council house, towers, and a theater, and was divided into four equally sized residential areas with the same layout. Roughly in the middle of the city stood a high citadel called the <a href="/wiki/Byrsa" title="Byrsa">Byrsa</a>.\n</p><p>Carthage was one of the largest cities of the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic period</a> and was among the largest cities in preindustrial history. Whereas by AD 14, Rome had at least 750,000 inhabitants and in the following century may have reached 1 million, the cities of Alexandria and Antioch numbered only a few hundred thousand or less.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CharlesworthEdwards2000_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-CharlesworthEdwards2000-11">[11]</a></sup> According to the not always reliable history of <a href="/wiki/Herodian" title="Herodian">Herodian</a>, Carthage rivaled Alexandria for second place in the Roman empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grant2004_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grant2004-12">[12]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maison_punique_byrsa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Maison_punique_byrsa.jpg/220px-Maison_punique_byrsa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Maison_punique_byrsa.jpg/330px-Maison_punique_byrsa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Maison_punique_byrsa.jpg/440px-Maison_punique_byrsa.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maison_punique_byrsa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Punic ruins in Byrsa</div></div></div>\n<p>On top of <a href="/wiki/Byrsa" title="Byrsa">Byrsa</a> hill, the location of the <a href="/wiki/Forum_(Roman)" title="Forum (Roman)">Roman Forum</a>, a residential area from the last century of existence (early second century BCE.) of the Punic city was excavated by the French archaeologist Serge Lancel. The neighborhood, with its houses, shops, and private spaces, is significant for what it reveals about daily life there over 2100 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The remains have been preserved under embankments, the substructures of the later Roman forum, whose foundation piles dot the district. The housing blocks are separated by a grid of straight streets about 6&#160;m (20&#160;ft) wide, with a roadway consisting of clay; <i><a href="/wiki/In_situ" title="In situ">in situ</a></i> stairs compensate for the slope of the hill. Construction of this type presupposes organization and political will, and has inspired the name of the neighborhood, "<a href="/wiki/Hannibal" title="Hannibal">Hannibal</a> district", referring to the legendary Punic general or <a href="/wiki/Shophet" title="Shophet">sufet</a> (consul) at the beginning of the second century BCE.\n</p><p>The habitat is typical, even stereotypical. The street was often used as a storefront/shopfront; cisterns were installed in basements to collect water for domestic use, and a long corridor on the right side of each residence led to a courtyard containing a <a href="/wiki/Sump" title="Sump">sump</a>, around which various other elements may be found. In some places, the ground is covered with mosaics called punica pavement, sometimes using a characteristic red mortar.\n</p><p>The merchant harbor at Carthage was developed, after settlement of the nearby Punic town of <a href="/wiki/Utica,_Tunisia" title="Utica, Tunisia">Utica</a>. Eventually the surrounding countryside was brought into the orbit of the Punic urban centers, first commercially, then politically. Direct management over cultivation of neighbouring lands by Punic owners followed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> A 28-volume work on agriculture written in Punic by <a href="/wiki/Mago_(agricultural_writer)" title="Mago (agricultural writer)">Mago</a>, a retired army general (c. 300), was translated into Latin and later into Greek. The original and both translations have been lost; however, some of Mago\'s text has survived in other Latin works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olive_trees" title="Olive trees">Olive trees</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Grafting" title="Grafting">grafting</a>), fruit trees (<a href="/wiki/Pomegranate" title="Pomegranate">pomegranate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Almond" title="Almond">almond</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ficus" title="Ficus">fig</a>, <a href="/wiki/Date_palm" title="Date palm">date palm</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viniculture" title="Viniculture">viniculture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bee" title="Bee">bees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">sheep</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poultry" title="Poultry">poultry</a>, implements, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Farm_management" title="Farm management">farm management</a> were among the ancient topics which Mago discussed. As well, Mago addresses the wine-maker\'s art (here a type of <a href="/wiki/Sherry" title="Sherry">sherry</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In Punic farming society, according to Mago, the small estate owners were the chief producers. They were, two modern historians write, not absent landlords. Rather, the likely reader of Mago was "the master of a relatively modest estate, from which, by great personal exertion, he extracted the maximum yield." Mago counselled the rural landowner, for the sake of their own \'utilitarian\' interests, to treat carefully and well their managers and farm workers, or their overseers and slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Yet elsewhere these writers suggest that rural land ownership provided also a new power base among the city\'s nobility, for those resident in their country villas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> By many, farming was viewed as an alternative endeavour to an urban business. Another modern historian opines that more often it was the urban merchant of Carthage who owned rural farming land to some profit, and also to retire there during the heat of summer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> It may seem that Mago anticipated such an opinion, and instead issued this contrary advice (as quoted by the Roman writer Columella):\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>"The man who acquires an estate must sell his house, lest he prefer to live in the town rather than in the country. Anyone who prefers to live in a town has no need of an estate in the country."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> "One who has bought land should sell his town house, so that he will have no desire to worship the household gods of the city rather than those of the country; the man who takes greater delight in his city residence will have no need of a country estate."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>The issues involved in rural land management also reveal underlying features of Punic society, its structure and <a href="/wiki/Social_stratification" title="Social stratification">stratification</a>. The hired workers might be considered \'rural proletariat\', drawn from the local Berbers. Whether or not there remained Berber landowners next to Punic-run farms is unclear. Some Berbers became sharecroppers. Slaves acquired for farm work were often prisoners of war. In lands outside Punic political control, independent Berbers cultivated grain and raised horses on their lands. Yet within the Punic domain that surrounded the city-state of Carthage, there were ethnic divisions in addition to the usual quasi <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feudal" title="Feudal">feudal</a> distinctions between lord and peasant, or master and serf. This inherent instability in the countryside drew the unwanted attention of potential invaders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Yet for long periods Carthage was able to manage these social difficulties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The many <a href="/wiki/Amphora" title="Amphora">amphorae</a> with Punic markings subsequently found about ancient Mediterranean coastal settlements testify to Carthaginian trade in locally made <a href="/wiki/Olive_oil" title="Olive oil">olive oil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wine" title="Wine">wine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Carthage\'s agricultural production was held in high regard by the ancients, and rivaled that of Rome&#8212;they were once competitors, e.g., over their olive harvests. Under Roman rule, however, <a href="/wiki/Grain" title="Grain">grain</a> production ([wheat] and <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a>) for export increased dramatically in \'Africa\'; yet these later fell with the rise in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Roman_Egypt" title="History of Roman Egypt">Roman Egypt</a>\'s grain exports. Thereafter olive groves and vineyards were re-established around Carthage. Visitors to the several growing regions that surrounded the city wrote admiringly of the lush green gardens, orchards, fields, <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a> channels, hedgerows (as boundaries), as well as the many prosperous farming towns located across the rural landscape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Accordingly, the Greek author and compiler <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus Siculus</a> (fl. 1st century BCE), who enjoyed access to ancient writings later lost, and on which he based most of his writings, described agricultural land near the city of Carthage circa 310 BC:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>"It was divided into market gardens and orchards of all sorts of fruit trees, with many streams of water flowing in channels irrigating every part. There were country homes everywhere, lavishly built and covered with stucco. ... Part of the land was planted with vines, part with olives and other productive trees. Beyond these, cattle and sheep were pastured on the plains, and there were meadows with grazing horses."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>The <i>Chora</i> (farm lands of Carthage) encompassed a limited area: the north coastal <i>tell</i>, the lower <a href="/wiki/Medjerda_River" title="Medjerda River">Bagradas river valley</a> (inland from Utica), <a href="/wiki/Cape_Bon" title="Cape Bon">Cape Bon</a>, and the adjacent <i>sahel</i> on the east coast. Punic culture here achieved the introduction of agricultural sciences first developed for lands of the eastern Mediterranean, and their adaptation to local African conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <i>urban landscape</i> of Carthage is known in part from ancient authors,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> augmented by modern digs and surveys conducted by archeologists. The "first urban nucleus" dating to the seventh century, in area about 10 hectares (25 acres), was apparently located on low-lying lands along the coast (north of the later harbors). As confirmed by archaeological excavations, Carthage was a "creation <i>ex nihilo</i>", built on \'virgin\' land, and situated at what was then the end of a peninsula. Here among "mud brick walls and beaten clay floors" (recently uncovered) were also found extensive cemeteries, which yielded evocative grave goods like clay masks. "Thanks to this burial archaeology we know more about archaic Carthage than about any other contemporary city in the western Mediterranean." Already in the eighth century, fabric <a href="/wiki/Dyeing" title="Dyeing">dyeing</a> operations had been established, evident from crushed shells of <a href="/wiki/Murex" title="Murex">murex</a> (from which the \'Phoenician purple\' was derived). Nonetheless, only a "meager picture" of the cultural life of the earliest pioneers in the city can be conjectured, and not much about housing, monuments or defenses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The Roman poet <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a> (70&#8211;19 BC) imagined early Carthage, when his legendary character <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Aeneid" title="The Aeneid">Aeneas</a> had arrived there:\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:357px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carthage.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="410" data-file-width="355" decoding="async" height="410" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0d/Carthage.png" width="355"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carthage.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Walled city-state of Carthage, before its fiery fall in 146 B.C.</div></div></div>\n<blockquote>\n<p>"Aeneas found, where lately huts had been,<br/>\nmarvelous buildings, gateways, cobbled ways,<br/>\nand din of wagons. There the <a href="/wiki/Tyre,_Lebanon" title="Tyre, Lebanon">Tyrians</a><br/>\nwere hard at work: laying courses for walls,<br/>\nrolling up stones to build the citadel,<br/>\nwhile others picked out building sites and plowed<br/>\na boundary furrow. Laws were being enacted,<br/>\nmagistrates and a sacred senate chosen.<br/>\nHere men were dredging harbors, there they laid<br/>\nthe deep foundations of a theatre,<br/>\nand quarried massive pillars... ."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><br/>\n</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>The two inner harbours [called in Punic <i>cothon</i>] were located in the southeast; one being commercial, and the other for war. Their definite functions are not entirely known, probably for the construction, outfitting, or repair of ships, perhaps also loading and unloading cargo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Larger <a href="/wiki/Anchor" title="Anchor">anchorages</a> existed to the north and south of the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> North and west of the <i>cothon</i> were located several industrial areas, e.g., metalworking and pottery (e.g., for <a href="/wiki/Amphora" title="Amphora">amphora</a>), which could serve both inner harbours, and ships anchored to the south of the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>\n</p><p>About the <a href="/wiki/Byrsa" title="Byrsa">Byrsa</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Citadel" title="Citadel">citadel</a> area to the north,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> considering its importance our knowledge of it is patchy. Its prominent heights were the scene of fierce combat during the fiery destruction of the city in 146 BC. The Byrsa was the reported site of the Temple of <a href="/wiki/Eshmun" title="Eshmun">Eshmun</a> (the healing god), at the top of a stairway of sixty steps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> A temple of <a href="/wiki/Tanit" title="Tanit">Tanit</a> (the city\'s queen goddess) was likely situated on the slope of the \'lesser Byrsa\' immediately to the east, which runs down toward the sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Also situated on the Byrsa were luxury homes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>\n</p><p>South of the citadel, near the <i>cothon</i> (the inner harbours) was the <i><a href="/wiki/Tophet" title="Tophet">tophet</a></i>, a special and very old <a href="/wiki/Cemetery" title="Cemetery">cemetery</a>, which when begun lay outside the city\'s boundaries. Here the <i>Salammb&#244;</i> was located, the <i>Sanctuary of Tanit</i>, not a temple but an enclosure for placing stone <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stelae" title="Stelae">stelae</a>. These were mostly short and upright, carved for funeral purposes. The presence of infant skeletons from here may indicate the occurrence of child sacrifice, as claimed in the Bible, although there has been considerable doubt among archeologists as to this interpretation and many consider it simply a cemetery devoted to infants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Probably the <i>tophet</i> burial fields were "dedicated at an early date, perhaps by the first settlers."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Recent studies, on the other hand, indicate that child sacrifice was practiced by the Carthaginians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sacrifice1_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-sacrifice1-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sacrifice2_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-sacrifice2-6">[6]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Between the sea-filled <i>cothon</i> for shipping and the Byrsa heights lay the <i><a href="/wiki/Agora" title="Agora">agora</a></i> [Greek: "market"], the city-state\'s central marketplace for business and commerce. The <i>agora</i> was also an area of public squares and plazas, where the people might formally assemble, or gather for festivals. It was the site of religious shrines, and the location of whatever were the major municipal buildings of Carthage. Here beat the heart of civic life. In this district of the Carthage, more probably, the ruling <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suffet" title="Suffet">suffets</a> presided, the council of elders convened, the tribunal of the 104 met, and justice was dispensed at trials in the open air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Early residential districts wrapped around the Byrsa from the south to the north east. Houses usually were <a href="/wiki/Whitewash" title="Whitewash">whitewashed</a> and blank to the street, but within were <a href="/wiki/Courtyard" title="Courtyard">courtyards</a> open to the sky.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> In these neighborhoods multistory construction later became common, some up to six stories tall according to an ancient Greek author.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Several <a href="/wiki/History_of_architecture" title="History of architecture">architectural</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Floorplan" title="Floorplan">floorplans</a> of homes have been revealed by recent <a href="/wiki/Excavation_(archaeology)" title="Excavation (archaeology)">excavations</a>, as well as the general layout of several <a href="/wiki/Urban_planning" title="Urban planning">city blocks</a>. Stone stairs were set in the streets, and <a href="/wiki/Drainage" title="Drainage">drainage</a> was planned, e.g., in the form of soakways leaching into the sandy soil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Along the Byrsa\'s southern slope were located not only fine old homes, but also many of the earliest grave-sites, juxtaposed in small areas, interspersed with daily life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Artisan" title="Artisan">Artisan</a> workshops were located in the city at sites north and west of the harbours. The location of three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metal_working" title="Metal working">metal workshops</a> (implied from iron slag and other vestiges of such activity) were found adjacent to the naval and commercial harbours, and another two were further up the hill toward the Byrsa citadel. Sites of <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">pottery</a> <a href="/wiki/Kiln" title="Kiln">kilns</a> have been identified, between the <i>agora</i> and the harbours, and further north. Earthenware often used Greek models. A <a href="/wiki/Fulling" title="Fulling">fuller</a>\'s shop for preparing woolen cloth (shrink and thicken) was evidently situated further to the west and south, then by the edge of the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Carthage also produced objects of rare refinement. During the 4th and 3rd centuries, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sculptures" title="Sculptures">sculptures</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Sarcophagus" title="Sarcophagus">sarcophagi</a> became works of art. "Bronze <a href="/wiki/Engraving" title="Engraving">engraving</a> and stone-carving reached their zenith."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The elevation of the land at the promontory on the seashore to the north-east (now called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sidi_Bou_Sa%C3%AFd" title="Sidi Bou Sa&#239;d">Sidi Bou Sa&#239;d</a>), was twice as high above sea level as that at the Byrsa (100 m and 50 m). In between runs a ridge, several times reaching 50 m; it continues northwestward along the seashore, and forms the edge of a plateau-like area between the Byrsa and the sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Newer urban developments lay here in these northern districts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Surrounding Carthage were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City_walls" title="City walls">walls</a> "of great strength" said in places to rise above 13 m, being nearly 10 m thick, according to ancient authors. To the west, three parallel walls were built. The walls altogether ran for about 33 kilometres (21 miles) to encircle the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> The heights of the Byrsa were additionally <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fortified" title="Fortified">fortified</a>; this area being the last to succumb to the <a href="/wiki/Third_Punic_War" title="Third Punic War">Romans in 146 BC</a>. Originally the Romans had landed their army on the strip of land extending southward from the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_history">Ancient history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carthage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Ancient history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Carthage" title="History of Carthage">History of Carthage</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dishekel_hispano-cartagin%C3%A9s-2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="196" data-file-width="422" decoding="async" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Dishekel_hispano-cartagin%C3%A9s-2.jpg/300px-Dishekel_hispano-cartagin%C3%A9s-2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f0/Dishekel_hispano-cartagin%C3%A9s-2.jpg 1.5x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dishekel_hispano-cartagin%C3%A9s-2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Carthaginian_currency" title="Carthaginian currency">Carthaginian</a> <a href="/wiki/Shekel" title="Shekel">shekel</a>, dated 237-227 BC, depicting the Punic god <a href="/wiki/Melqart" title="Melqart">Melqart</a> (equivalent of <a href="/wiki/Hercules" title="Hercules">Hercules</a>/<a href="/wiki/Heracles" title="Heracles">Heracles</a>), most likely with the features of <a href="/wiki/Hamilcar_Barca" title="Hamilcar Barca">Hamilcar Barca</a>, father of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hannibal_Barca" title="Hannibal Barca">Hannibal Barca</a>; on the reverse is a man riding an elephant</div></div></div>\n<p>Greek cities contested with Carthage for the Western Mediterranean culminating in the <a href="/wiki/Sicilian_Wars" title="Sicilian Wars">Sicilian Wars</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pyrrhic_War" title="Pyrrhic War">Pyrrhic War</a> over <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>, while the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Romans</a> fought three wars against Carthage, known as the <a href="/wiki/Punic_Wars" title="Punic Wars">Punic Wars</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> "Punic" meaning "Phoenician" in Latin.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Punic_Republic">Punic Republic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carthage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Punic Republic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Carthage" title="Ancient Carthage">Ancient Carthage</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carthaginianempire.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2276" data-file-width="2424" decoding="async" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Carthaginianempire.PNG/250px-Carthaginianempire.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Carthaginianempire.PNG/375px-Carthaginianempire.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Carthaginianempire.PNG/500px-Carthaginianempire.PNG 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carthaginianempire.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Downfall of the Carthaginian Empire <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #0076ae; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Lost to Rome in the First Punic War <span class="nowrap">(264 &#8211;</span> <span class="nowrap">241 BC)</span></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #0fff4b; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Won after the First Punic War, lost in the Second Punic War</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #509bfd; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Lost in the Second Punic War <span class="nowrap">(218 &#8211;</span> <span class="nowrap">201 BC)</span></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #bf01fe; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Conquered by Rome in the Third Punic War <span class="nowrap">(149 &#8211;</span> <span class="nowrap">146 BC)</span></div></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carthage_Holdings.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3278" data-file-width="5016" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Carthage_Holdings.png/220px-Carthage_Holdings.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Carthage_Holdings.png/330px-Carthage_Holdings.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Carthage_Holdings.png/440px-Carthage_Holdings.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carthage_Holdings.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Carthaginian-held territory in the early 3rd century BC</div></div></div>\n<p>The Carthaginian republic was one of the longest-lived and largest states in the ancient Mediterranean. Reports relay several wars with Syracuse and finally, Rome, which eventually resulted in the defeat and destruction of Carthage in the Third Punic War. The Carthaginians were <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenician</a> settlers originating in the Mediterranean coast of the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>. They spoke <a href="/wiki/Canaanite_languages" title="Canaanite languages">Canaanite</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic language</a>, and followed a local variety of the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Canaanite_religion" title="Ancient Canaanite religion">ancient Canaanite religion</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tunisie_Carthage_Ruines_08.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Tunisie_Carthage_Ruines_08.JPG/220px-Tunisie_Carthage_Ruines_08.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Tunisie_Carthage_Ruines_08.JPG/330px-Tunisie_Carthage_Ruines_08.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Tunisie_Carthage_Ruines_08.JPG/440px-Tunisie_Carthage_Ruines_08.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tunisie_Carthage_Ruines_08.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ruins of Carthage</div></div></div>\n<p>The fall of Carthage came at the end of the Third Punic War in 146 BC at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Carthage_(c._149_BCE)" title="Battle of Carthage (c. 149 BCE)">Battle of Carthage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Despite initial devastating Roman naval losses and Rome\'s recovery from the brink of defeat after the terror of a 15-year occupation of much of Italy by <a href="/wiki/Hannibal" title="Hannibal">Hannibal</a>, the end of the series of wars resulted in the end of Carthaginian power and the complete destruction of the city by <a href="/wiki/Scipio_Aemilianus" title="Scipio Aemilianus">Scipio Aemilianus</a>. The Romans pulled the Phoenician warships out into the harbor and burned them before the city, and went from house to house, capturing and enslaving the people. About 50,000 Carthaginians were sold into <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_ancient_Rome" title="Slavery in ancient Rome">slavery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> The city was set ablaze and razed to the ground, leaving only ruins and rubble. After the fall of Carthage, Rome annexed the majority of the Carthaginian colonies, including other North African locations such as <a href="/wiki/Volubilis" title="Volubilis">Volubilis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lixus_(ancient_city)" title="Lixus (ancient city)">Lixus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chellah" title="Chellah">Chellah</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mogador" title="Mogador">Mogador</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The legend that the city was <a href="/wiki/Salting_the_earth" title="Salting the earth">sown with salt</a> remains widely accepted despite a lack of evidence among ancient historical accounts;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carthage_1988_pp._308_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carthage_1988_pp._308-71">[71]</a></sup> According to R.T. Ridley, the earliest such claim is attributable to B.L. Hallward\'s chapter in <i>Cambridge Ancient History</i>, published in 1930. Ridley contended that Hallward\'s claim may have gained traction due to historical evidence of other salted-earth instances such as <a href="/wiki/Abimelech" title="Abimelech">Abimelech</a>\'s salting of <a href="/wiki/Shechem" title="Shechem">Shechem</a> in <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Judges" title="Book of Judges">Judges</a> 9:45.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> B.H. Warmington admitted he had repeated Hallward\'s error, but posited that the legend precedes 1930 and inspired repetitions of the practice. He also suggested that it is useful to understand how subsequent historical narratives have been framed and that the symbolic value of the legend is so great and enduring that it mitigates a deficiency of concrete evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carthage_1988_pp._308_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carthage_1988_pp._308-71">[71]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Starting in the 19th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> various texts claim that after defeating the city of Carthage in the <a href="/wiki/Third_Punic_War" title="Third Punic War">Third Punic War</a> (146 BC), the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman</a> general <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scipio_Aemilianus_Africanus" title="Scipio Aemilianus Africanus">Scipio Aemilianus Africanus</a> ordered the city be sacked, forced its surviving inhabitants into <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a>, plowed it over and sowed it with salt. However, no ancient sources exist documenting the salting itself. The element of salting is therefore probably a later invention modeled on the Biblical story of <a href="/wiki/Shechem" title="Shechem">Shechem</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> The ritual of symbolically drawing a <a href="/wiki/Plough" title="Plough">plow</a> over the site of a city is mentioned in ancient sources, but not in reference to Carthage specifically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> When <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boniface_VIII" title="Boniface VIII">Pope Boniface VIII</a> destroyed <a href="/wiki/Palestrina" title="Palestrina">Palestrina</a> in 1299, he issued a <a href="/wiki/Papal_bull" title="Papal bull">papal bull</a> that it be plowed "following the old example of Carthage in Africa" and also salted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> "I have run the plough over it, like the ancient Carthage of Africa, and I have had salt sown upon it...."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_Carthage">Roman Carthage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carthage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Roman Carthage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Karta_Karthago.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="520" data-file-width="555" decoding="async" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Karta_Karthago.PNG/220px-Karta_Karthago.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Karta_Karthago.PNG/330px-Karta_Karthago.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Karta_Karthago.PNG/440px-Karta_Karthago.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Karta_Karthago.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Roman Carthage</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Roman_Carthage" title="Roman Carthage">Roman Carthage</a></div>\n<p>When Carthage fell, its nearby rival <a href="/wiki/Utica,_Tunisia" title="Utica, Tunisia">Utica</a>, a Roman ally, was made capital of the region and replaced Carthage as the leading center of Punic trade and leadership. It had the advantageous position of being situated on the outlet of the <a href="/wiki/Medjerda_River" title="Medjerda River">Medjerda River</a>, Tunisia\'s only river that flowed all year long. However, grain cultivation in the Tunisian mountains caused large amounts of <a href="/wiki/Silt" title="Silt">silt</a> to erode into the river. This silt accumulated in the harbor until it became useless, and Rome was forced to rebuild Carthage.\n</p><p>By 122 BC, <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Gracchus" title="Gaius Gracchus">Gaius Gracchus</a> founded a short-lived <a href="/wiki/Colonia_(Roman)" title="Colonia (Roman)">colony</a>, called <i><a href="/wiki/Colonia_Junonia" title="Colonia Junonia">Colonia Iunonia</a></i>, after the Latin name for the Punic goddess <a href="/wiki/Tanit" title="Tanit">Tanit</a>, <i>Iuno Caelestis</i>. The purpose was to obtain arable lands for impoverished farmers. The <a href="/wiki/Roman_Senate" title="Roman Senate">Senate</a> abolished the colony some time later, to undermine Gracchus\' power.\n</p><p>After this ill-fated attempt, a new city of Carthage was built on the same land by <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> in the period from 49 to 44 BC, and by the first century, it had grown to be the second-largest city in the western half of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, with a peak population of 500,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> It was the center of the <a href="/wiki/Africa_(Roman_province)" title="Africa (Roman province)">province of Africa</a>, which was a major breadbasket of the Empire. Among its major monuments was an <a href="/wiki/Carthage_amphitheatre" title="Carthage amphitheatre">amphitheater</a>.\n</p><p>Carthage also became a <a href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity#Carthage" title="Early centers of Christianity">center of early Christianity</a> (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carthage_(episcopal_see)" title="Carthage (episcopal see)">Carthage (episcopal see)</a>). In the first of a string of rather poorly reported councils at Carthage a few years later, no fewer than 70 bishops attended. <a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">Tertullian</a> later broke with the mainstream that was increasingly represented in the West by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primacy_of_the_Bishop_of_Rome" title="Primacy of the Bishop of Rome">primacy of the Bishop of Rome</a>, but a more serious rift among Christians was the <a href="/wiki/Donatism" title="Donatism">Donatist controversy</a>, which <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a> spent much time and parchment arguing against. At the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Council_of_Carthage_(397)" title="Council of Carthage (397)">Council of Carthage (397)</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Development_of_the_Christian_biblical_canon" title="Development of the Christian biblical canon">biblical canon for the western Church was confirmed</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NE_500ad.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1049" data-file-width="1424" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/NE_500ad.jpg/220px-NE_500ad.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/NE_500ad.jpg/330px-NE_500ad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/NE_500ad.jpg/440px-NE_500ad.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NE_500ad.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Vandal_Kingdom" title="Vandal Kingdom">Vandal Kingdom</a> in 500, centered on Carthage</div></div></div>\n<p>The political fallout from the deep disaffection of <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Africa" title="Christianity in Africa">African Christians</a> is supposedly a crucial factor in the ease with which Carthage and the other centers were captured in the fifth century by <a href="/wiki/Gaiseric" title="Gaiseric">Gaiseric</a>, king of the <a href="/wiki/Vandals" title="Vandals">Vandals</a>, who defeated the Roman general <a href="/wiki/Bonifacius" title="Bonifacius">Bonifacius</a> and made the city the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Vandal_Kingdom" title="Vandal Kingdom">Vandal Kingdom</a>. Gaiseric was considered a heretic, too, an <a href="/wiki/Arianism" title="Arianism">Arian</a>, and though Arians commonly despised <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic</a> Christians, a mere promise of toleration might have caused the city\'s population to accept him.\n</p><p>After a failed attempt to recapture the city in the fifth century, the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Eastern Roman Empire</a> finally subdued the Vandals in the <a href="/wiki/Vandalic_War" title="Vandalic War">Vandalic War</a> in 533&#8211;534. Thereafter, the city became the seat of the <a href="/wiki/Praetorian_prefecture_of_Africa" title="Praetorian prefecture of Africa">praetorian prefecture of Africa</a>, which was made into an <a href="/wiki/Exarchate" title="Exarchate">exarchate</a> during the emperor <a href="/wiki/Maurice_(emperor)" title="Maurice (emperor)">Maurice\'s</a> reign, as was <a href="/wiki/Ravenna" title="Ravenna">Ravenna</a> on the Italian Peninsula. These two exarchates were the western bulwarks of the Byzantine Empire, all that remained of its power in the West. In the early seventh century <a href="/wiki/Heraclius_the_Elder" title="Heraclius the Elder">Heraclius the Elder</a>, the exarch of Carthage, overthrew the Byzantine emperor <a href="/wiki/Phocas" title="Phocas">Phocas</a>, whereupon his son <a href="/wiki/Heraclius" title="Heraclius">Heraclius</a> succeeded to the imperial throne.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_period">Islamic period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carthage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Islamic period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_the_Maghreb" title="Muslim conquest of the Maghreb">Muslim conquest of the Maghreb</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Carthage_(698)" title="Battle of Carthage (698)">Battle of Carthage (698)</a></div>\n<p>The Roman <a href="/wiki/Exarchate_of_Africa" title="Exarchate of Africa">Exarchate of Africa</a> was not able to withstand the seventh-century <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_the_Maghreb" title="Muslim conquest of the Maghreb">Muslim conquest of the Maghreb</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad Caliphate</a> under <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Malik_ibn_Marwan" title="Abd al-Malik ibn Marwan">Abd al-Malik ibn Marwan</a> in 686 sent a force led by Zuhayr ibn Qais, who won a battle over the Romans and <a href="/wiki/Berbers" title="Berbers">Berbers</a> led by King <a href="/wiki/Kusaila" title="Kusaila">Kusaila</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Altava" title="Kingdom of Altava">Kingdom of Altava</a> on the plain of <a href="/wiki/Kairouan" title="Kairouan">Kairouan</a>, but he could not follow that up. In 695, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hasan_ibn_al-Nu%27man" title="Hasan ibn al-Nu\'man">Hasan ibn al-Nu\'man</a> captured Carthage and advanced into the <a href="/wiki/Atlas_Mountains" title="Atlas Mountains">Atlas Mountains</a>. An imperial fleet arrived and retook Carthage, but in 698, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hasan_ibn_al-Nu%27man" title="Hasan ibn al-Nu\'man">Hasan ibn al-Nu\'man</a> returned and defeated Emperor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tiberios_III" title="Tiberios III">Tiberios III</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Carthage_(698)" title="Battle of Carthage (698)">698 Battle of Carthage</a>. Roman imperial forces withdrew from all of Africa except <a href="/wiki/Ceuta" title="Ceuta">Ceuta</a>. Roman Carthage was destroyed&#8212;its walls torn down, its water supply cut off, and its harbors made unusable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> \nThe destruction of the Exarchate of Africa marked a permanent end to the Byzantine Empire\'s influence in the region.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Medina_of_Tunis" title="Medina of Tunis">Medina of Tunis</a>, originally a Berber settlement, was established as the new regional center under the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad Caliphate</a> in the early 8th century. Under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aghlabid" title="Aghlabid">Aghlabids</a>, the people of Tunis revolted numerous times, but the city profited from economic improvements and quickly became the second most important in the kingdom. It was briefly the national capital, from the end of the reign of <a href="/wiki/Ibrahim_II_of_Ifriqiya" title="Ibrahim II of Ifriqiya">Ibrahim II</a> in 902, until 909, when the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shi%27ite" title="Shi\'ite">Shi\'ite</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berber_people" title="Berber people">Berbers</a> took over <a href="/wiki/Ifriqiya" title="Ifriqiya">Ifriqiya</a> and founded the <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_Caliphate" title="Fatimid Caliphate">Fatimid Caliphate</a>.\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carthage_(episcopal_see)" title="Carthage (episcopal see)">Carthage</a> remained a residential see until the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_medieval_period" title="High medieval period">high medieval period</a>, mentioned in\ntwo letters of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_IX" title="Pope Leo IX">Pope Leo IX</a> dated 1053,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> written in reply to consultations regarding a conflict between the bishops of Carthage and <a href="/wiki/Africa_(Roman_province)#Episcopal_sees" title="Africa (Roman province)">Gummi</a>.\nIn each of the two letters, Pope Leo declares that, after the Bishop of Rome, the first archbishop and chief metropolitan of the whole of <a href="/wiki/Africa_(Roman_province)" title="Africa (Roman province)">Africa</a> is the bishop of Carthage. \nLater, an archbishop of Carthage named Cyriacus was imprisoned by the Arab rulers because of an accusation by some Christians. <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_VII" title="Pope Gregory VII">Pope Gregory VII</a> wrote him a letter of consolation, repeating the hopeful assurances of the primacy of the Church of Carthage, "whether the Church of Carthage should still lie desolate or rise again in glory". \nBy 1076, Cyriacus was set free, but there was only one other bishop in the province. These are the last of whom there is mention in that period of the history of the see.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_history">Modern history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carthage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Modern history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plan_Tunis_et_ses_environs_-_1903.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1308" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="288" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Plan_Tunis_et_ses_environs_-_1903.jpg/220px-Plan_Tunis_et_ses_environs_-_1903.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Plan_Tunis_et_ses_environs_-_1903.jpg/330px-Plan_Tunis_et_ses_environs_-_1903.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Plan_Tunis_et_ses_environs_-_1903.jpg/440px-Plan_Tunis_et_ses_environs_-_1903.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plan_Tunis_et_ses_environs_-_1903.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Historical map of the Tunis area (1903), showing <a href="/wiki/Acropolium_of_Carthage" title="Acropolium of Carthage">St. Louis of Carthage</a> between <a href="/wiki/Sidi_Bou_Said" title="Sidi Bou Said">Sidi Bou Said</a> and <a href="/wiki/Le_Kram" title="Le Kram">Le Kram</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Carthage is some 15 kilometres (9.3 miles) east-northeast of Tunis; the settlements nearest to Carthage were the town of <a href="/wiki/Sidi_Bou_Said" title="Sidi Bou Said">Sidi Bou Said</a> to the north and the village of <a href="/wiki/Le_Kram" title="Le Kram">Le Kram</a> to the south. \nSidi Bou Saint was a village which had grown around the tomb of the eponymous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sufi" title="Sufi">sufi</a> saint (d. 1231), which had been developed into a town under <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Tunisia" title="Ottoman Tunisia">Ottoman rule</a> in the 18th century. Le Kram was developed in the late 19th century under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_Tunisia" title="French Tunisia">French administration</a> as a settlement close to the port of <a href="/wiki/La_Goulette" title="La Goulette">La Goulette</a>.\n</p><p>In 1881, Tunisia became a <a href="/wiki/French_protectorate_of_Tunisia" title="French protectorate of Tunisia">French protectorate</a>, and in the same year <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lavigerie" title="Charles Lavigerie">Charles Lavigerie</a>, who was archbishop of Algiers, became <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apostolic_administrator" title="Apostolic administrator">apostolic administrator</a> of the vicariate of Tunis. In the following year, Lavigerie became a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardinalate" title="Cardinalate">cardinal</a>. He "saw himself as the reviver of the ancient Christian Church of Africa, the Church of Cyprian of Carthage",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> and, on 10 November 1884, was successful in his great ambition of having the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metropolitan_see" title="Metropolitan see">metropolitan see</a> of Carthage restored, with himself as its first archbishop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> In line with the declaration of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_IX" title="Pope Leo IX">Pope Leo IX</a> in 1053, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_XIII" title="Pope Leo XIII">Pope Leo XIII</a> acknowledged the revived Archdiocese of Carthage as the <a href="/wiki/Primate_(bishop)" title="Primate (bishop)">primatial</a> see of <a href="/wiki/Africa_(Roman_province)" title="Africa (Roman province)">Africa</a> and Lavigerie as primate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Acropolium_of_Carthage" title="Acropolium of Carthage">Acropolium of Carthage</a> (Saint Louis Cathedral of Carthage) was erected on <a href="/wiki/Byrsa" title="Byrsa">Byrsa</a> hill in 1884.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Archaeological_site">Archaeological site</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carthage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Archaeological site">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Danish consul <a href="/wiki/Christian_Tuxen_Falbe" title="Christian Tuxen Falbe">Christian Tuxen Falbe</a> conducted a first survey of the topography of the archaeological site (published in 1833).\nAntiquarian interest was intensified following the publication of Flaubert\'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salammb%C3%B4_(opera)" title="Salammb&#244; (opera)">Salammb&#244;</a></i> in 1858. <a href="/wiki/Charles_Ernest_Beul%C3%A9" title="Charles Ernest Beul&#233;">Charles Ernest Beul&#233;</a> performed some preliminary excavations of Roman remains on Byrsa hill in 1860.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> A more systematic survey of both Punic and Roman-era remains is due to <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Louis_Delattre" title="Alfred Louis Delattre">Alfred Louis Delattre</a>, who was sent to Tunis by cardinal <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lavigerie" title="Charles Lavigerie">Charles Lavigerie</a> in 1875 on both an apostolic and an archaeological mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>\nAudollent (1901, <a class="external text" href="http://www.mediterranee-antique.fr/Auteurs/Fichiers/ABC/Audollent/Carthage/Cart_203.htm" rel="nofollow">p. 203</a>) cites Delattre and Lavigerie to the effect that in the 1880s, locals still knew the area of the ancient city under the name of <i>Cartagenna</i> (i.e. reflecting the Latin <i>n</i>-stem <i>Carth&#257;gine</i>).\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Auguste_Audollent" title="Auguste Audollent">Auguste Audollent</a> divides the area of Roman Carthage into four quarters, <i>Cartagenna</i>, <i>Derm&#232;che</i>, <i>Byrsa</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Cisterns_of_La_Malga" title="Cisterns of La Malga">La Malga</a></i>. Cartagenna and Derm&#232;che correspond with the lower city, including the site of Punic Carthage; Byrsa is associated with the upper city, which in Punic times was a walled citadel above the harbour; and <i>La Malga</i> is linked with the more remote parts of the upper city in Roman times.\n</p><p>French-led excavations at Carthage began in 1921, and from 1923 reported finds of a large quantity of urns containing a mixture of animal and children\'s bones. <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Dussaud" title="Ren&#233; Dussaud">Ren&#233; Dussaud</a> identified a 4th-century BC stela found in Carthage as depicting a child sacrifice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A temple at <a href="/wiki/Amman" title="Amman">Amman</a> (1400&#8211;1250 BC) excavated and reported upon by <a href="/wiki/Basil_Hennessy" title="Basil Hennessy">J.B. Hennessy</a> in 1966, shows the possibility of bestial and human sacrifice by fire. While evidence of child sacrifice in Canaan was the object of academic disagreement, with some scholars arguing that merely children\'s cemeteries had been unearthed in Carthage, the mixture of children\'s with animal bones as well as associated epigraphic evidence involving mention of <i>mlk</i> led to a consensus that, at least in Carthage, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Child_sacrifice_in_Cartaginian_religion" title="Child sacrifice in Cartaginian religion">child sacrifice</a> was indeed common practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2016, an ancient Carthaginian individual, who was excavated from a Punic tomb in Byrsa Hill, was found to belong to the rare <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_U_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup U (mtDNA)">U5b2c1</a> maternal haplogroup. The Young Man of Byrsa specimen dates from the late 6th century BCE, and his lineage is believed to represent early gene flow from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iberia" title="Iberia">Iberia</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Commune">Commune</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carthage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Commune">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carthage_(commune)" title="Carthage (commune)">Carthage (commune)</a></div>\n<p>In 1920, the first <a href="/wiki/Seaplane" title="Seaplane">seaplane</a> base was built on the <a href="/wiki/Lake_of_Tunis" title="Lake of Tunis">Lake of Tunis</a> for the seaplanes of Compagnie A&#233;ronavale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BonnichonG&#233;ny2012_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-BonnichonG&#233;ny2012-93">[93]</a></sup> The Tunis Airfield opened in 1938, serving around 5,800 passengers annually on the Paris-<a href="/wiki/Tunis" title="Tunis">Tunis</a> route.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Staff1954_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Staff1954-94">[94]</a></sup> \nDuring World War II, the airport was used by the United States Army <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_Force" title="Air Force">Air Force</a> <a href="/wiki/Twelfth_Air_Force" title="Twelfth Air Force">Twelfth Air Force</a> as a headquarters and command control base for the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Campaign_(World_War_II)" title="Italian Campaign (World War II)">Italian Campaign</a> of 1943. \nConstruction on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tunis-Carthage_Airport" title="Tunis-Carthage Airport">Tunis-Carthage Airport</a>, which was fully funded by France, began in 1944, and in 1948 the airport become the main hub for <a href="/wiki/Tunisair" title="Tunisair">Tunisair</a>.\n</p><p>In the 1950s the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lyc%C3%A9e_Fran%C3%A7ais_de_Carthage&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lyc&#233;e Fran&#231;ais de Carthage (page does not exist)">Lyc&#233;e Fran&#231;ais de Carthage</a> was established to serve French families in Carthage. In 1961 it was given to the Tunisian government as part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Independence_of_Tunisia" title="Independence of Tunisia">Independence of Tunisia</a>, so the nearby Coll&#232;ge Maurice Cailloux in <a href="/wiki/La_Marsa" title="La Marsa">La Marsa</a>, previously an annex of the Lyc&#233;e Fran&#231;ais de Carthage, was renamed to the Lyc&#233;e Fran&#231;ais de La Marsa and began serving the <i>lyc&#233;e</i> level. It is currently the <a href="/wiki/Lyc%C3%A9e_Gustave_Flaubert_(La_Marsa)" title="Lyc&#233;e Gustave Flaubert (La Marsa)">Lyc&#233;e Gustave Flaubert</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quisommenous_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Quisommenous-95">[95]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After Tunisian independence in 1956, the Tunis conurbation gradually extended around the airport, and Carthage (&#1602;&#1585;&#1591;&#1575;&#1580; <i> Qar&#7789;&#257;j</i>) is now a suburb of Tunis, covering the area between Sidi Bou Said and Le Kram.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RingSalkin1996_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-RingSalkin1996-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>\nIts population as of January 2013 was estimated at 21,276,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> \nmostly attracting the more wealthy residents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> If Carthage is not the capital, it tends to be the political pole, a &#171;&#160;place of emblematic power&#160;&#187; according to <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sophie_Bessis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sophie Bessis (page does not exist)">Sophie Bessis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bessis_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-bessis-100">[100]</a></sup> leaving to Tunis the economic and administrative roles. The <a href="/wiki/Carthage_Palace" title="Carthage Palace">Carthage Palace</a> (the Tunisian presidential palace) is located in the coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The suburb has six train stations of the <a href="/wiki/TGM" title="TGM">TGM</a> line between Le Kram and Sidi Bou Said: \nCarthage Salammbo (named for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salambo" title="Salambo">Salambo</a>, the fictional daughter of Hamilcar), Carthage Byrsa (named for <a href="/wiki/Byrsa" title="Byrsa">Byrsa</a> hill), Carthage Dermech (<i>Derm&#232;che</i>), Carthage Hannibal (named for <a href="/wiki/Hannibal" title="Hannibal">Hannibal</a>), Carthage Pr&#233;sidence (named for the <a href="/wiki/Carthage_Palace" title="Carthage Palace">Presidential Palace</a>) and Carthage Amilcar (named for <a href="/wiki/Hamilcar" title="Hamilcar">Hamilcar</a>).\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade_and_business">Trade and business</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carthage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Trade and business">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:218BCMAPMEDITERRANEAN.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1215" data-file-width="2052" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/218BCMAPMEDITERRANEAN.jpg/220px-218BCMAPMEDITERRANEAN.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/218BCMAPMEDITERRANEAN.jpg/330px-218BCMAPMEDITERRANEAN.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/218BCMAPMEDITERRANEAN.jpg/440px-218BCMAPMEDITERRANEAN.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:218BCMAPMEDITERRANEAN.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the Mediterranean in 218 BC</div></div></div>\n<p>The merchants of Carthage were in part heirs of the Mediterranean trade developed by Phoenicia, and so also heirs of the rivalry with Greek merchants. Business activity was accordingly both stimulated and challenged. <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> had been an early site of such commercial contests. The Phoenicians then had ventured into the western Mediterranean, founding trading posts, including Utica and Carthage. The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greeks</a> followed, entering the western seas where the commercial rivalry continued. Eventually it would lead, especially in <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>, to several centuries of intermittent war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Although Greek-made merchandise was generally considered superior in design, Carthage also produced trade goods in abundance. That Carthage came to function as a manufacturing colossus was shown during the <a href="/wiki/Third_Punic_War" title="Third Punic War">Third Punic War</a> with Rome.
